<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Human body</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Entire structure of a human being</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Anatomy of the human body" redirects here. For the textbook, see <a href="/wiki/Gray%27s_Anatomy" title="Gray's Anatomy">Gray's Anatomy</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_Body_02.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9791" data-file-width="5121" decoding="async" height="421" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Human_Body_02.png/220px-Human_Body_02.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Human_Body_02.png/330px-Human_Body_02.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Human_Body_02.png/440px-Human_Body_02.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_Body_02.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Female (left) and male (right) adult human bodies photographed in ventral (above) and dorsal (below) perspectives. Naturally-occurring <a href="/wiki/Pubic_hair" title="Pubic hair">pubic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Body_hair" title="Body hair">body</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Facial_hair" title="Facial hair">facial hair</a> has been <a href="/wiki/Hair_removal" title="Hair removal">deliberately removed</a> to show anatomy</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>human body</b> is the structure of a <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human being</a>. It is composed of many different types of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> that together create <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> and subsequently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_systems" title="Organ systems">organ systems</a>. They ensure <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">viability</a> of the human body.
</p><p>It comprises a <a href="/wiki/Human_head" title="Human head">head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">neck</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trunk_(anatomy)" title="Trunk (anatomy)">trunk</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Thorax" title="Thorax">thorax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdomen</a>), <a href="/wiki/Arm" title="Arm">arms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hands" title="Hands">hands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_leg" title="Human leg">legs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feet" title="Feet">feet</a>.
</p><p>The study of the human body involves <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryology</a>. The body <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical_variability" title="Anatomical variability">varies anatomically</a> in known ways. Physiology focuses on the systems and organs of the human body and their functions. Many systems and mechanisms interact in order to maintain <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, with safe levels of substances such as sugar and oxygen in the blood.
</p><p>The body is studied by <a href="/wiki/Health_professional" title="Health professional">health professionals</a>, physiologists, anatomists, and by artists to assist them in their work.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable floatright" style="font-size: 86%;">

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">human body is composed of</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> including <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AboutChemical_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-AboutChemical-1">[1]</a></sup> These elements reside in trillions of cells and non-cellular components of the body.
</p><p>The adult male body is about 60% <a href="/wiki/Body_water" title="Body water">water</a> for a total water content of some 42 litres (9.2 imp gal; 11 US gal). This is made up of about 19 litres (4.2 imp gal; 5.0 US gal) of extracellular fluid including about 3.2 litres (0.70 imp gal; 0.85 US gal) of blood plasma and about 8.4 litres (1.8 imp gal; 2.2 US gal) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstitial_fluid" title="Interstitial fluid">interstitial fluid</a>, and about 23 litres (5.1 imp gal; 6.1 US gal) of fluid inside cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The content, acidity and composition of the water inside and outside cells is carefully maintained. The main electrolytes in body water outside cells are <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>, whereas within cells it is <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGanong's20165_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGanong's20165-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_distinct_cell_types_in_the_adult_human_body" title="List of distinct cell types in the adult human body">List of distinct cell types in the adult human body</a></div>
<p>The body contains trillions of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, the fundamental unit of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> At maturity, there are roughly 30<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sender-et-al_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-sender-et-al-5">[5]</a></sup>–37<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><span class="nowrap"> </span>trillion cells in the body, an estimate arrived at by totaling the cell numbers of all the <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organs</a> of the body and <a href="/wiki/List_of_distinct_cell_types_in_the_adult_human_body" title="List of distinct cell types in the adult human body">cell types</a>. The body is also host to about the same number of non-human cells<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sender-et-al_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-sender-et-al-5">[5]</a></sup> as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular organisms</a> which reside in the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> and on the skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fredericks_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fredericks-7">[7]</a></sup> Not all parts of the body are made from cells. Cells sit in an <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a> that consists of proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a>, surrounded by extracellular fluids. Of the 70 kg (150 lb) weight of an average human body, nearly 25 kg (55 lb) is non-human cells or non-cellular material such as bone and connective tissue.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome">Genome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Genome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">Genome</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetics</a></div>
<p>Cells in the body function because of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. DNA sits within the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus of a cell</a>. Here, parts of DNA are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">copied</a> and sent to the body of the cell via <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGanong's201616_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGanong's201616-8">[8]</a></sup> The RNA is then used to <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">create</a> <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> which form the basis for cells, their activity, and their products. Proteins dictate cell function and gene expression, a cell is able to self-regulate by the amount of proteins produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> However, not all cells have DNA; some cells such as mature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_blood_cells" title="Red blood cells">red blood cells</a> lose their nucleus as they mature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tissues">Tissues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tissues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox" style="width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg"><img alt="2120 Major Systemic Artery.jpg" data-file-height="2275" data-file-width="1404" decoding="async" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg/210px-2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg/315px-2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg/420px-2120_Major_Systemic_Artery.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Ae4MadKPJC0" rel="nofollow">Human Body 101</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic" title="National Geographic">National Geographic</a></i>, 5:10</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The body consists of many different types of <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a>, defined as cells that act with a specialised function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The study of tissues is called <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a> and often occurs with a <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a>. The body consists of four main types of tissues. These are lining cells (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epithelia" title="Epithelia">epithelia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_tissue" title="Nerve tissue">nerve tissue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tissue" title="Muscle tissue">muscle tissue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy200827_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy200827-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cells that lie on surfaces exposed to the outside world or gastrointestinal tract (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epithelia" title="Epithelia">epithelia</a>) or internal cavities (<a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">endothelium</a>) come in numerous shapes and forms – from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squamous_epithelium" title="Squamous epithelium">single layers of flat cells</a>, to cells with small beating hair-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a> in the lungs, to column-like cells that line the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>. Endothelial cells are cells that line internal cavities including blood vessels and glands. Lining cells regulate what can and can't pass through them, protect internal structures, and function as sensory surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy200827_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray's_Anatomy200827-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organs">Organs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Organs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_organs_of_the_human_body" title="List of organs of the human body">List of organs of the human body</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">Organs</a>, structured collections of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> with a specific function,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> mostly sit within the body, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Human_skin" title="Human skin">skin</a>. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>. Many organs reside within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_body_cavities" title="Human body cavities">cavities</a> within the body. These cavities include the <a href="/wiki/Abdominopelvic_cavity" title="Abdominopelvic cavity">abdomen</a> (which contains the stomach, for example) and <a href="/wiki/Pleural_cavity" title="Pleural cavity">pleura</a>, which contains the lungs.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart">Heart</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Heart">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">Heart</a></div>
<p>The heart is an organ located in the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_cavity" title="Thoracic cavity">thoracic cavity</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> and slightly to the left. It is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Pericardium" title="Pericardium">pericardium</a> which holds it in place in the <a href="/wiki/Mediastinum" title="Mediastinum">mediastinum</a> and serves to protect it from blunt trauma, <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> and help lubricate the movement of the heart via <a href="/wiki/Pericardial_fluid" title="Pericardial fluid">pericardial fluid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The heart works by <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">pumping blood around the body</a> allowing <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">waste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> to be transported. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_the_human_heart_(cropped).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="663" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/207px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/311px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/414px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 2x" width="207"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_the_human_heart_(cropped).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the human heart</div></div></div>
<p>The heart is composed of <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">two atrium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">two ventricles</a>. The primary purpose of the atrium is to allow uninterrupted venous blood flow to the heart during <a href="/wiki/Systole" title="Systole">ventricular systole</a>. This allows enough blood to get into the ventricles during <a href="/wiki/Systole" title="Systole">atrial systole</a>. A lack of the atrium would cause a <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_output" title="Cardiac output">decrease in cardiac output</a> of 75%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The purpose of the ventricles is to <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pump blood to the lungs</a> through the right ventricle and to <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">the rest of the body</a> through the left ventricle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The heart has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conduction_system_of_the_heart" title="Electrical conduction system of the heart">electrical conduction system</a> to control the contractions and relaxation of the muscle. It starts in the <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">sinoatrial node</a> traveling through the atria causing them to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrial_systole" title="Atrial systole">pump blood into the ventricles</a>. It then travels to the <a href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_node" title="Atrioventricular node">atrioventricular node</a> which makes the signal slow down slightly allowing the ventricles to fill with blood before pumping it out and starting the cycle over again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_disease" title="Coronary artery disease">Coronary artery disease</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_causes_of_death_by_rate" title="List of causes of death by rate">leading cause of death worldwide</a>, making up 16% of all deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It is caused by the buildup of <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">plaque</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Coronary_arteries" title="Coronary arteries">coronary arteries</a> supplying the heart, eventually the arteries may become so narrow that <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">not enough blood</a> is able to reach the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">myocardium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> a condition known as <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">myocardial infarction or heart attack</a>, this can cause <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a> and eventually death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Risk factors for coronary artery disease include <a href="/wiki/Obesity-associated_morbidity" title="Obesity-associated morbidity">obesity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_tobacco" title="Health effects of tobacco">smoking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolemia" title="Hypercholesterolemia">high cholesterol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">lack of exercise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heart_cancer" title="Heart cancer">Cancer can affect the heart</a>, though it is exceedingly rare and has usually <a href="/wiki/Metastasis" title="Metastasis">metastasized</a> from another part of the body such as the <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lungs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breasts</a>. This is because the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle" title="Cardiac muscle">heart cells</a> quickly stop dividing and all growth occurs through <a href="/wiki/Hypertrophy" title="Hypertrophy">size increase</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">cell division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallbladder">Gallbladder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Gallbladder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">Gallbladder</a></div>
<p>The gallbladder is a hollow pear shaped organ located <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posterior_(anatomy)" title="Posterior (anatomy)">posterior</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inferior_(anatomy)" title="Inferior (anatomy)">inferior</a> middle part of the <a href="/wiki/Lobes_of_liver" title="Lobes of liver">right lobe of the liver</a>. It is variable in shape and size. It stores <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a> before it is released into the small intestine via the <a href="/wiki/Common_bile_duct" title="Common bile duct">common bile duct</a> to help with <a href="/wiki/Lipid_metabolism" title="Lipid metabolism">digestion of fats</a>. It receives bile from the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Cystic_duct" title="Cystic duct">cystic duct</a> which connects to the <a href="/wiki/Common_hepatic_duct" title="Common hepatic duct">common hepatic duct</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Common_bile_duct" title="Common bile duct">common bile duct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagral2005_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nagral2005-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gallbladder gets its blood supply from the <a href="/wiki/Cystic_artery" title="Cystic artery">cystic artery</a> which in most people emerges from the <a href="/wiki/Hepatic_artery_proper" title="Hepatic artery proper">right hepatic artery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagral2005_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nagral2005-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gallstone" title="Gallstone">Gallstones</a> is a common disease in which one or more stones form in the gallbladder or <a href="/wiki/Biliary_tract" title="Biliary tract">biliary tract</a>. Most people are asymptomatic but if a stone blocks the biliary tract it causes a <a href="/wiki/Biliary_colic" title="Biliary colic">gallbladder attack</a>, symptoms may include sudden pain in the upper right abdomen and or center of the abdomen. Nausea and vomiting may also occur. Typical treatment is removal of the gallbladder through a procedure called a <a href="/wiki/Cholecystectomy" title="Cholecystectomy">cholecystectomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Having gallstones is a risk factor for <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder_cancer" title="Gallbladder cancer">gallbladder cancer</a> which although quite uncommon is rapidly fatal if not diagnosed early.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_systems_of_the_human_body" title="List of systems of the human body">List of systems of the human body</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Circulatory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">Circulatory system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> consists of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillaries</a>). The heart propels the circulation of the blood, which serves as a "transportation system" to transfer <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, fuel, nutrients, waste products, immune cells and signalling molecules (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormones" title="Hormones">hormones</a>) from one part of the body to another. Paths of blood circulation within the human body can be divided into two circuits: the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circuit</a>, which pumps blood to the lungs to receive <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and leave <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, and the systemic circuit, which carries blood from the heart off to the rest of the body. The blood consists of fluid that carries <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> in the circulation, including some that move from tissue to blood vessels and back, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestive_system">Digestive system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Digestive system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">Digestive system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="581" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg/220px-Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg/330px-Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg/440px-Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Digestive_system_diagram_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Digestive system</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a> consists of the mouth including the <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teeth_(human)" title="Teeth (human)">teeth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a>, <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small</a> and <a href="/wiki/Large_intestine" title="Large intestine">large intestines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a>), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a>. It converts food into small, nutritional, non-toxic <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> for distribution and absorption into the body. These molecules take the form of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> (which are broken down into <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>), <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> (the last of which are mainly ionic rather than molecular). After being <a href="/wiki/Swallowing" title="Swallowing">swallowed</a>, food moves through the <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristalsis</a>: the systematic expansion and contraction of muscles to push food from one area to the next.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niddk.nih.gov_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-niddk.nih.gov-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Digestion begins in the <a href="/wiki/Mouth" title="Mouth">mouth</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Chewing" title="Chewing">chews</a> food into smaller pieces for easier digestion. Then it is <a href="/wiki/Swallowing" title="Swallowing">swallowed</a>, and moves through the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>. In the stomach, food is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Gastric_acid" title="Gastric acid">gastric acids</a> to allow the extraction of <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>. What is left is called <a href="/wiki/Chyme" title="Chyme">chyme</a>; this then moves into the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a>, which absorbs the nutrients and water from the chyme. What remains passes on to the <a href="/wiki/Large_intestine" title="Large intestine">large intestine</a>, where it is dried to form <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>; these are then stored in the <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a> until they are expelled through the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niddk.nih.gov_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-niddk.nih.gov-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Endocrine_system">Endocrine system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Endocrine system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">Endocrine system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine system</a> consists of the principal <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_gland" title="Endocrine gland">endocrine glands</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pituitary" title="Pituitary">pituitary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adrenal" title="Adrenal">adrenals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parathyroid" title="Parathyroid">parathyroids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a>, but nearly all organs and tissues produce specific endocrine <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> as well. The endocrine hormones serve as signals from one body system to another regarding an enormous array of conditions, and resulting in variety of changes of function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immune_system">Immune system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Immune system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">Immune system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> consists of the <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymph_node" title="Lymph node">lymph nodes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymph</a> channels, which are also part of the <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">lymphatic system</a>. The immune system provides a mechanism for the body to distinguish its own cells and tissues from outside cells and substances and to neutralize or destroy the latter by using specialized proteins such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cytokines" title="Cytokines">cytokines</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptors" title="Toll-like receptors">toll-like receptors</a>, among many others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skin_layers.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1034" data-file-width="1505" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Skin_layers.svg/236px-Skin_layers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Skin_layers.svg/354px-Skin_layers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Skin_layers.svg/472px-Skin_layers.svg.png 2x" width="236"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skin_layers.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skin</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Integumentary_system">Integumentary system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Integumentary system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Integumentary_system" title="Integumentary system">Integumentary system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Integumentary_system" title="Integumentary system">integumentary system</a> consists of the covering of the body (the skin), including hair and <a href="/wiki/Nail_(anatomy)" title="Nail (anatomy)">nails</a> as well as other functionally important structures such as the <a href="/wiki/Sweat_gland" title="Sweat gland">sweat glands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sebaceous_gland" title="Sebaceous gland">sebaceous glands</a>. The skin provides containment, structure, and protection for other organs, and serves as a major sensory interface with the outside world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lymphatic_system">Lymphatic system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lymphatic system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">Lymphatic system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">lymphatic system</a> extracts, transports and metabolizes lymph, the fluid found in between cells. The lymphatic system is similar to the circulatory system in terms of both its structure and its most basic function, to carry a body fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Musculoskeletal_system">Musculoskeletal system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Musculoskeletal system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_system" title="Musculoskeletal system">Musculoskeletal system</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_system" title="Musculoskeletal system">musculoskeletal system</a> consists of the <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">human skeleton</a> (which includes <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joints" title="Joints">joints</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a>) and attached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>. It gives the body basic structure and the ability for movement. In addition to their structural role, the larger bones in the body contain <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a>, the site of production of blood cells. Also, all bones are major storage sites for <a href="/wiki/Calcium_in_biology" title="Calcium in biology">calcium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>. This system can be split up into the <a href="/wiki/Muscular_system" title="Muscular system">muscular system</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal_system" title="Skeletal system">skeletal system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">Nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">Nervous system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:185px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nervous_system_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="695" data-file-width="421" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nervous_system_diagram.png/183px-Nervous_system_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nervous_system_diagram.png/275px-Nervous_system_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nervous_system_diagram.png/367px-Nervous_system_diagram.png 2x" width="183"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nervous_system_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nervous system</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> consists of the body's <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glia" title="Glia">glial</a> cells, which together form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerves" title="Nerves">nerves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganglia" title="Ganglia">ganglia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gray_matter" title="Gray matter">gray matter</a> which in turn form the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> and related structures. The brain is the organ of <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thought</a>, emotion, <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sensory_processing" title="Sensory processing">sensory processing</a>; it serves many aspects of communication and controls various systems and functions. The <a href="/wiki/Special_senses" title="Special senses">special senses</a> consist of <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">vision</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearing_(sense)" title="Hearing (sense)">hearing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">smell</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">eyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">ears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tongue" title="Tongue">tongue</a>, and nose gather information about the body's environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>From a structural perspective, the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> is typically subdivided into two component parts: the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> (CNS), composed of the brain and the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS), composed of the nerves and <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a> outside the brain and spinal cord. The CNS is mostly responsible for organizing <a href="/wiki/Motion" title="Motion">motion</a>, processing <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensory information</a>, thought, memory, <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> and other such functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livescience.com_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-livescience.com-39">[39]</a></sup> It remains a matter of some debate whether the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">CNS</a> directly gives rise to <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a> (PNS) is mostly responsible for gathering information with <a href="/wiki/Sensory_neuron" title="Sensory neuron">sensory neurons</a> and directing body movements with <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livescience.com_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-livescience.com-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>From a functional perspective, the nervous system is again typically divided into two component parts: the <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">somatic nervous system</a> (SNS) and the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a> (ANS). The SNS is involved in voluntary functions like <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">speaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">sensory processes</a>. The ANS is involved in involuntary processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> and regulating <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nervous system is subject to many different diseases. In <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, abnormal electrical activity in the brain can cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">seizures</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Multiple_sclerosis" title="Multiple sclerosis">multiple sclerosis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> attacks the <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">nerve linings</a>, damaging the nerves' ability to transmit signals. <a href="/wiki/Amyotrophic_lateral_sclerosis" title="Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis">Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis</a> (ALS), also known as <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a>'s disease, is a <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neuron</a> disease which gradually reduces movement in patients. There are also many other diseases of the nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livescience.com_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-livescience.com-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_system">Reproductive system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reproductive system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_reproductive_system" title="Human reproductive system">Human reproductive system</a></div>
<p>The primary purpose of the reproductive system is to produce new humans in the form of <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a> and ensure their <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">sexual development</a> so that they can produce new humans too. It is composed of the <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a> (<a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a>) which produce <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">eggs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">sperm cells</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a>) and produce <a href="/wiki/Sex_hormone" title="Sex hormone">hormones</a> necessary for proper <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">sexual development</a>. The rest of the <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a> and ducts of the reproductive system are responsible for the transportation and sustaining of the <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">nurture the offspring</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reproductive system is first stimulated by the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> during <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> which causes the <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testes</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Testosterone" title="Testosterone">testosterone</a> (male) and <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> (female). Male puberty generally occurs between the ages of 13-15 and is characterized by beginning of <a href="/wiki/Spermatogenesis" title="Spermatogenesis">sperm production</a> and the development of <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">secondary sex characteristics</a> such as increased height and weight, broadened shoulders, <a href="/wiki/Pubic_hair" title="Pubic hair">pubic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Facial_hair" title="Facial hair">facial hair</a>, voice deepening and muscle development. Female puberty generally occurs between the ages of 9-13 and is characterized by <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>. The growth of secondary sex characteristics such as growth of <a href="/wiki/Pubic_hair" title="Pubic hair">pubic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underarm_hair" title="Underarm hair">underarm hair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breast" title="Breast">breast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vaginal</a> growth, widening hips and increased height and weight also occur during the female puberty process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="557" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg/360px-Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg/540px-Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg/720px-Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_male_reproductive_system_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internal <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">gross anatomy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Male_reproductive_system" title="Male reproductive system">male reproductive system</a></div></div></div>
<p>The external <a href="/wiki/Male_reproductive_system" title="Male reproductive system">male reproductive system</a> is made up of the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scrotum" title="Scrotum">scrotum</a> which is a bag that protects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Testis" title="Testis">testis</a>. The penis consists of the <a href="/wiki/Glans_penis" title="Glans penis">glans</a> which is the head of the penis and contains the <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urinary_meatus" title="Urinary meatus">urinary meatus</a> the point where urine exits the penis, the rest of the penis is called the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_cavernosum_penis" title="Corpus cavernosum penis">shaft or corpus cavernosum</a> and contains most of the <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a>. The glans is covered by a fold of skin called the <a href="/wiki/Foreskin" title="Foreskin">foreskin</a> though this can be removed through <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png/220px-Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png/330px-Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png/440px-Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mons-pubis-anatomical-location-in-female-reproductive-system.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>External female reproductive system with mons pubis highlighted</div></div></div>
<p>Internally the male reproductive system starts in the testes where hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Seminiferous_tubule" title="Seminiferous tubule">seminiferous tubules</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> which is then stored and matured into <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Epididymis" title="Epididymis">epididymis</a>. They are then brought through the <a href="/wiki/Vas_deferens" title="Vas deferens">vas deferens</a> which leads away from the testes to the <a href="/wiki/Seminal_vesicles" title="Seminal vesicles">seminal vesicles</a> where the sperm cells are mixed with a <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>-rich fluid from the seminal vesicles allowing the sperm to stay alive and remain healthy. It is then brought by the <a href="/wiki/Ejaculatory_duct" title="Ejaculatory duct">ejaculatory duct</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Prostate" title="Prostate">prostate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulbourethral_gland" title="Bulbourethral gland">bulbourethral gland</a>, and mixed with fluids from said glands. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostatic_fluid" title="Prostatic fluid">fluid from the prostate</a> helps to neutralize the acidity of the vagina and keep the sperm alive. This mixture of sperm and accessory gland fluids is called <a href="/wiki/Semen" title="Semen">semen</a> and is released through the <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a> during <a href="/wiki/Ejaculation" title="Ejaculation">ejaculation</a>. Almost the entire internal male reproductive system works in pairs with two <a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testes</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Epididymis" title="Epididymis">epididymis</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Vas_deferens" title="Vas deferens">vas deferens</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Seminal_vesicles" title="Seminal vesicles">seminal vesicles</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Ejaculatory_duct" title="Ejaculatory duct">ejaculatory ducts</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Bulbourethral_gland" title="Bulbourethral gland">bulbourethral glands</a>, but only one <a href="/wiki/Prostate" title="Prostate">prostate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The external <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">female reproductive system</a> also called the <a href="/wiki/Vulva" title="Vulva">vulva</a> consists of the <a href="/wiki/Mons_pubis" title="Mons pubis">mons pubis</a> a fatty mass that covers the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Labia_majora" title="Labia majora">labia majora</a> (outer lips of the vagina), the <a href="/wiki/Labia_minora" title="Labia minora">labia minora</a> (inner lips of the vagina), the <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vaginal opening</a> which opens into vagina and is where the penis is inserted during <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">children are born</a>, the urethral opening which is the opening for the <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a> that carries <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Clitoris" title="Clitoris">clitoris</a> which contains the most sensitive nerve endings and in humans has no use other than <a href="/wiki/Sexual_stimulation" title="Sexual stimulation">sexual pleasure</a>. Located between the anus and vagina is the <a href="/wiki/Perineum" title="Perineum">perineum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="567" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/220px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/330px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg/440px-Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scheme_female_reproductive_system-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internal <a href="/wiki/Gross_anatomy" title="Gross anatomy">gross anatomy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">female reproductive system</a></div></div></div>
<p>Internally the female reproductive system contains two <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">ovaries</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">uterus</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">fallopian tubes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cervix" title="Cervix">cervix</a>. At birth a female has about 700,000 <a href="/wiki/Oocyte" title="Oocyte">oocytes</a> (the immature version of the egg cell) in both ovaries combined, though this degenerates to about 400,000 by the time <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> is reached. This is a lifetime supply as after birth no more <a href="/wiki/Oocyte" title="Oocyte">oocytes</a> are produced, compared to males where <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">sperm cells</a> are produced during their entire lifetime. During puberty the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a> begins for the first time, in response to low <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> levels the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> releases <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Gonadotropin-releasing hormone">gonadotropin releasing hormone</a> (GnRH). This causes the <a href="/wiki/Anterior_pituitary" title="Anterior pituitary">anterior pituitary gland</a> to release <a href="/wiki/Follicle-stimulating_hormone" title="Follicle-stimulating hormone">follicle-stimulating hormone</a> (FSH) and <a href="/wiki/Luteinizing_hormone" title="Luteinizing hormone">luteinizing hormone</a> (LH). FSH stimulates <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">ovarian follicles</a> to grow and one dominant follicle will eventually take over. As it continues to grow it releases more and more <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">estradiol</a> which stimulates LH secretion and suppresses FSH secretion preventing further follicular growth. When <a href="/wiki/Luteinizing_hormone" title="Luteinizing hormone">LH levels are highest</a> the follicle ruptures releasing the <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">ovum</a> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulation</a> where it is then moved to one of the <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">fallopian tubes</a>. After ovulation the portion of the follicle that remains in the ovary is transformed into <a href="/wiki/Corpus_luteum" title="Corpus luteum">corpus luteum</a> which continues to produce estrogen and high levels of progesterone. The <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> causes the <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">endometrium</a> to grow thick preparing it for implantation of a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">fertilized egg</a>. If fertilization occurs the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_luteum" title="Corpus luteum">corpus luteum</a> continues to secrete hormones until the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> develops enough to secrete the necessary hormones for maintaining <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>. Eventually the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_luteum" title="Corpus luteum">corpus luteum</a> will turn into <a href="/wiki/Corpus_albicans" title="Corpus albicans">corpus albicans</a> which is essentially scar tissue. If fertilization fails the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_luteum" title="Corpus luteum">corpus luteum</a> will degrade into <a href="/wiki/Corpus_albicans" title="Corpus albicans">corpus albicans</a> and stop secreting enough <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">progesterone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a> causing the <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">endometrial lining</a> to break resulting in <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">menstruation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many diseases affect the reproductive system such as <a href="/wiki/Polycystic_ovary_syndrome" title="Polycystic ovary syndrome">polycystic ovary syndrome</a> (PCOS) which is characterized by <a href="/wiki/Hyperandrogenism" title="Hyperandrogenism">elevated androgen levels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irregular_menstruation" title="Irregular menstruation">menstrual irregularities</a> and/or small <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cyst" title="Ovarian cyst">cysts</a> on one or both of the ovaries. It is a common disease affecting at least 7% of adult women. Symptoms include excess body hair, <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weight_gain" title="Weight gain">weight gain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pattern_hair_loss" title="Pattern hair loss">male pattern baldness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irregular_menstruation" title="Irregular menstruation">irregular menstruation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another reproductive disease is <a href="/wiki/Testicular_torsion" title="Testicular torsion">testicular torsion</a>, it occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Spermatic_cord" title="Spermatic cord">spermatic cord</a> which holds up the testicle wraps around itself cutting off <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">blood flow to the testicle</a>. <a href="/wiki/Testicular_torsion" title="Testicular torsion">Testicular torsion</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Medical_emergency" title="Medical emergency">medical emergency</a> and can result in testicular death or <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a> if not treated immediately by physically unwrapping the <a href="/wiki/Spermatic_cord" title="Spermatic cord">spermatic cord</a>. If the testicle has suffered <a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">severe damage</a>, it may need to be surgically in a lateral <a href="/wiki/Orchiectomy" title="Orchiectomy">orchiectomy</a>. The primary symptom of testicular torsion is severe <a href="/wiki/Testicular_pain" title="Testicular pain">testicular pain</a> at rest, nausea and vomiting may also occur. Testicular torsion can occur at any age though it is most common in 12-18 year olds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">Sexually transmitted infections</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlamydia" title="Chlamydia">chlamydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_papillomavirus_infection" title="Human papillomavirus infection">HPV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genital_wart" title="Genital wart">genital warts</a> are spread through <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> including <a href="/wiki/Oral_sex" title="Oral sex">oral</a>, vaginal and <a href="/wiki/Anal_sex" title="Anal sex">anal sex</a>. Many of these infections can be lethal if left untreated though others are mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benignity" title="Benignity">harmless</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cancer can affect many parts of the reproductive system including <a href="/wiki/Penile_cancer" title="Penile cancer">the penis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Testicular_cancer" title="Testicular cancer">testicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_cancer" title="Vaginal cancer">vagina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube_cancer" title="Fallopian tube cancer">fallopian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uterine_cancer" title="Uterine cancer">uterus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vulvar_cancer" title="Vulvar cancer">vulva</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_system">Respiratory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Respiratory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">Respiratory system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a> consists of the nose, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasopharynx" title="Nasopharynx">nasopharynx</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>. It brings oxygen from the air and excretes <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water back into the air. First, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">air</a> is pulled through the trachea into the lungs by the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a> pushing down, which creates a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>. Air is briefly stored inside small sacs known as <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_alveolus" title="Pulmonary alveolus">alveoli</a> (sing.: alveolus) before being expelled from the lungs when the diaphragm contracts again. Each alveolus is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillaries</a> carrying deoxygenated blood, which absorbs oxygen out of the air and into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodstream" title="Bloodstream">bloodstream</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd.com_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-webmd.com-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the respiratory system to function properly, there need to be as few impediments as possible to the movement of air within the lungs. <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">Inflammation</a> of the lungs and excess <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> are common sources of breathing difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd.com_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-webmd.com-55">[55]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, the respiratory system is persistently inflamed, causing <a href="/wiki/Wheeze" title="Wheeze">wheezing</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">Pneumonia</a> occurs through <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> of the alveoli, and may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">Emphysema</a>, commonly a result of <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>, is caused by damage to connections between the alveoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Urinary_system">Urinary system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Urinary system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urinary_system" title="Urinary system">Urinary system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urinary_System_(Female).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png/220px-Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png/330px-Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png/440px-Urinary_System_%28Female%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urinary_System_(Female).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Female urinary system</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Urinary_system" title="Urinary system">urinary system</a> consists of the two <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Ureter" title="Ureter">ureters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a>. It removes waste materials from the blood through urine, which carries a variety of waste molecules and excess <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> and water out of the body.
</p><p>First, the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> filter the blood through their respective <a href="/wiki/Nephron" title="Nephron">nephrons</a>, removing <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">waste products</a> like <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Creatinine" title="Creatinine">creatinine</a> and maintaining the <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte_imbalance" title="Electrolyte imbalance">proper balance</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a> and turning the waste products into <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> by combining them with water from the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The kidneys filter about 150 quarts (170 liters) of blood daily, but most of it is returned to the blood stream with only 1-2 quarts (1-2 liters) ending up as urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The urine is brought by the <a href="/wiki/Ureter" title="Ureter">ureters</a> from the kidneys down to the <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> lining the ureter walls continuously tighten and relax through a process called <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristalsis</a> to force urine away from the kidneys and down into the bladder. Small amounts of urine are released into the bladder every 10–15 seconds.
</p><p>The bladder is a hollow balloon shaped organ located in the <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a>. It stores urine until the brain signals it to relax the <a href="/wiki/Urethral_sphincters" title="Urethral sphincters">urinary sphincter</a> and release the urine into the urethra starting <a href="/wiki/Urination" title="Urination">urination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A normal bladder can hold up to 16 ounces (half a liter) for 3–5 hours comfortably.
</p><p>Numerous <a href="/wiki/Urologic_disease" title="Urologic disease">diseases affect the urinary system</a> including <a href="/wiki/Kidney_stone_disease" title="Kidney stone disease">kidney stones</a> which are formed when materials in the urine concentrate enough to form a solid mass, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infections</a> which are infections of the urinary tract and can cause pain when urinating, frequent urination and even death if left untreated. <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">Renal failure</a> occurs when the kidneys fail to adequately filter waste from the blood and can lead to death if not treated with <a href="/wiki/Kidney_dialysis" title="Kidney dialysis">dialysis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kidney_transplantation" title="Kidney transplantation">kidney transplantation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">Cancer</a> can affect the <a href="/wiki/Bladder_cancer" title="Bladder cancer">bladder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney_cancer" title="Kidney cancer">kidneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urethral_cancer" title="Urethral cancer">urethra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ureteral_cancer" title="Ureteral cancer">ureters</a> with the latter two being far more rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_body&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Anatomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_human_anatomy" title="Outline of human anatomy">Outline of human anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">Anatomy</a></div>
